<p class="mw-empty-elt">

<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 6</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Carbon,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">6</span></sub>C</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_spectrum_visible.png" title="Color lines in a spectral range"><img alt="Color lines in a spectral range" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="7430" height="41" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Carbon_spectrum_visible.png/240px-Carbon_spectrum_visible.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Carbon_spectrum_visible.png/360px-Carbon_spectrum_visible.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Carbon_spectrum_visible.png/480px-Carbon_spectrum_visible.png 2x" width="240"/></a></div></div><strong><a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">Spectral lines</a> of carbon</strong></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#f0ff8f">Other properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">Crystal structure</a></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_carbon" title="Template:Infobox carbon"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_carbon" title="Template talk:Infobox carbon"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_carbon&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Carbon</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">carbo</i> "coal") is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with <a href="/wiki/Symbol_(chemistry)" title="Symbol (chemistry)">symbol</a> <b>C</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 6. It is <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetallic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetravalence" title="Tetravalence">tetravalent</a>—making four <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> available to form <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a>. It belongs to group 14 of the periodic table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Three <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_carbon" title="Isotopes of carbon">isotopes</a> occur naturally, <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12"><sup>12</sup>C</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13"><sup>13</sup>C</a> being stable, while <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14"><sup>14</sup>C</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclide</a>, decaying with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of about 5,730 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isotopes_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-isotopes-14">[14]</a></sup> Carbon is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discoveries_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Discoveries of the chemical elements">few elements known since antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-D2-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon is the 15th <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">most abundant element in the Earth's crust</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">fourth most abundant element in the universe by mass</a> after <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>. Carbon's abundance, its unique diversity of <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>, and its unusual ability to form <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a> at the temperatures commonly encountered on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> enables this element to serve as a common element of <a href="/wiki/Carbon-based_life" title="Carbon-based life">all known life</a>. It is the second most abundant element in the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">human body</a> by mass (about 18.5%) after oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The atoms of carbon can bond together in different ways, termed <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_carbon" title="Allotropes of carbon">allotropes of carbon</a>. The best known are <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-therm_prop_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-therm_prop-17">[17]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Physical_property" title="Physical property">physical properties</a> of carbon vary widely with the allotropic form. For example, graphite is <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">opaque</a> and black while diamond is highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transparency_(optics)" title="Transparency (optics)">transparent</a>. Graphite is soft enough to form a streak on paper (hence its name, from the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> verb "γράφειν" which means "to write"), while diamond is the hardest naturally occurring material known. Graphite is a good <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductor</a> while diamond has a low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>. Under normal conditions, diamond, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotubes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a> have the highest <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivities</a> of <a href="/wiki/List_of_thermal_conductivities" title="List of thermal conductivities">all known materials</a>. All carbon allotropes are solids under normal conditions, with graphite being the most <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamically stable</a> form at standard temperature and pressure. They are chemically resistant and require high temperature to react even with oxygen.
</p><p>The most common <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> of carbon in <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compounds</a> is +4, while +2 is found in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl" title="Metal carbonyl">carbonyl</a> complexes. The largest sources of inorganic carbon are <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, but significant quantities occur in organic deposits of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane clathrates</a>. Carbon forms a vast number of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>, more than any other element, with almost ten million compounds described to date,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanl_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-18">[18]</a></sup> and yet that number is but a fraction of the number of theoretically possible compounds under standard conditions. For this reason, carbon has often been referred to as the "king of the elements".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-phase-diagramp.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="467" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Carbon-phase-diagramp.svg/260px-Carbon-phase-diagramp.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Carbon-phase-diagramp.svg/390px-Carbon-phase-diagramp.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Carbon-phase-diagramp.svg/520px-Carbon-phase-diagramp.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-phase-diagramp.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Theoretically predicted phase diagram of carbon</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_carbon" title="Allotropes of carbon">allotropes of carbon</a> include <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, one of the softest known substances, and <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>, the hardest naturally occurring substance. It <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bonds</a> readily with other small <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, including other carbon atoms, and is capable of forming multiple stable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent" title="Covalent">covalent</a> bonds with suitable multivalent atoms. Carbon is known to form almost ten million different compounds, a large majority of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_compounds" title="Chemical compounds">chemical compounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanl_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-18">[18]</a></sup> Carbon also has the highest <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a> point of all elements. At <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> it has no melting point, as its <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> is at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7007108000000000000♠</span>10.8<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.2 MPa</span> and 4,600 ± 300 K (4,330 ± 300 °C; 7,820 ± 540 °F),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-triple2_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-triple2-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-triple3_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-triple3-3">[3]</a></sup> so it sublimes at about 3,900 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-triple_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-triple-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Graphite is much more reactive than diamond at standard conditions, despite being more thermodynamically stable, as its delocalised <a href="/wiki/Pi_bond" title="Pi bond">pi system</a> is much more vulnerable to attack. For example, graphite can be oxidised by hot concentrated <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> at standard conditions to <a href="/wiki/Mellitic_acid" title="Mellitic acid">mellitic acid</a>, C<sub>6</sub>(CO<sub>2</sub>H)<sub>6</sub>, which preserves the hexagonal units of graphite while breaking up the larger structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood289_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood289-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon sublimes in a carbon arc, which has a temperature of about 5800 K (5,530 °C or 9,980 °F). Thus, irrespective of its allotropic form, carbon remains solid at higher temperatures than the highest-melting-point metals such as <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a>. Although thermodynamically prone to <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidation</a>, carbon resists oxidation more effectively than elements such as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, which are weaker reducing agents at room temperature.
</p><p>Carbon is the sixth element, with a ground-state <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> of 1s<sup>2</sup>2s<sup>2</sup>2p<sup>2</sup>, of which the four outer electrons are <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>. Its first four ionisation energies, 1086.5, 2352.6, 4620.5 and 6222.7 kJ/mol, are much higher than those of the heavier group-14 elements. The electronegativity of carbon is 2.5, significantly higher than the heavier group-14 elements (1.8–1.9), but close to most of the nearby nonmetals, as well as some of the second- and third-row <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a>. Carbon's <a href="/wiki/Covalent_radius" title="Covalent radius">covalent radii</a> are normally taken as 77.2 pm (C−C), 66.7 pm (C=C) and 60.3 pm (C≡C), although these may vary depending on coordination number and what the carbon is bonded to. In general, covalent radius decreases with lower coordination number and higher bond order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood276_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood276-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon compounds form the basis of all known life on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">carbon–nitrogen cycle</a> provides some of the energy produced by the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>. Although it forms an extraordinary variety of compounds, most forms of carbon are comparatively unreactive under normal conditions. At standard temperature and pressure, it resists all but the strongest oxidizers. It does not react with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> or any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" title="Alkali metals">alkalis</a>. At elevated temperatures, carbon reacts with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Oxocarbon" title="Oxocarbon">carbon oxides</a> and will rob oxygen from metal oxides to leave the elemental metal. This <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic reaction</a> is used in the iron and steel industry to <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelt</a> iron and to control the carbon content of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 4 C<sub>(s)</sub> → 3 Fe<sub>(s)</sub> + 4 CO<sub>(g)</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Carbon monoxide can be recycled to smelt even more iron:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 4 CO<sub>(g)</sub> → 3 Fe<sub>(s)</sub> + 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span><sub>(g)</sub></dd></dl>
<p>with <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a> and with steam in the coal-gas reaction:
</p>
<dl><dd>C<sub>(s)</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>(g)</sub> → CO<sub>(g)</sub> + H<sub>2(g)</sub>.</dd></dl>
<p>Carbon combines with some metals at high temperatures to form metallic carbides, such as the iron carbide <a href="/wiki/Cementite" title="Cementite">cementite</a> in steel and <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_carbide" title="Tungsten carbide">tungsten carbide</a>, widely used as an <a href="/wiki/Abrasive" title="Abrasive">abrasive</a> and for making hard tips for cutting tools.
</p><p>The system of carbon allotropes spans a range of extremes:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<td>Graphite is one of the softest materials known.
</td>
<td style="width: 50%;">Synthetic <a href="/wiki/Aggregated_diamond_nanorod" title="Aggregated diamond nanorod">nanocrystalline diamond</a> is the hardest material known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Graphite is a very good <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricant</a>, displaying <a href="/wiki/Superlubricity" title="Superlubricity">superlubricity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>Diamond is the ultimate <a href="/wiki/Abrasive" title="Abrasive">abrasive</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Graphite is a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conductor</a> of electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>Diamond is an excellent electrical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insulator_(electrical)" title="Insulator (electrical)">insulator</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and has the highest breakdown electric field of any known material.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Some forms of graphite are used for <a href="/wiki/Thermal_insulation" title="Thermal insulation">thermal insulation</a> (i.e. firebreaks and heat shields), but some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrolytic_graphite" title="Pyrolytic graphite">other forms</a> are good thermal conductors.
</td>
<td>Diamond is the best known naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/List_of_thermal_conductivities" title="List of thermal conductivities">thermal conductor</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Graphite is <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">opaque</a>.
</td>
<td>Diamond is highly transparent.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Graphite crystallizes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_(crystal_system)" title="Hexagonal (crystal system)">hexagonal system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>Diamond crystallizes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubic_(crystal_system)" title="Cubic (crystal system)">cubic system</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Amorphous carbon is completely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a>.
</td>
<td>Carbon nanotubes are among the most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anisotropic" title="Anisotropic">anisotropic</a> materials known.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allotropes">Allotropes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_carbon" title="Allotropes of carbon">Allotropes of carbon</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Atomic_carbon" title="Atomic carbon">Atomic carbon</a> is a very short-lived species and, therefore, carbon is stabilized in various multi-atomic structures with different molecular configurations called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotropes" title="Allotropes">allotropes</a>. The three relatively well-known allotropes of carbon are <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>. Once considered exotic, <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerenes</a> are nowadays commonly synthesized and used in research; they include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buckyball" title="Buckyball">buckyballs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buckyballs_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-buckyballs-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nanotubes_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nanotubes-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotubes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nanotubes2_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-nanotubes2-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanobud" title="Carbon nanobud">carbon nanobuds</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nanobuds_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-nanobuds-32">[32]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_nanofibers" title="Carbon nanofibers">nanofibers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Several other exotic allotropes have also been discovered, such as <a href="/wiki/Lonsdaleite" title="Lonsdaleite">lonsdaleite</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lonsdaletite_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-lonsdaletite-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glassy_carbon" title="Glassy carbon">glassy carbon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glassy_carbon_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-glassy_carbon-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanofoam" title="Carbon nanofoam">carbon nanofoam</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Linear_acetylenic_carbon" title="Linear acetylenic carbon">linear acetylenic carbon</a> (carbyne).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAC_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-LAC-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">Graphene</a> is a two-dimensional sheet of carbon with the atoms arranged in a hexagonal lattice. As of 2009, graphene appears to be the strongest material ever tested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-lee-39">[39]</a></sup> The process of separating it from <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> will require some further technological development before it is economical for industrial processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nypost_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-nypost-40">[40]</a></sup> If successful, graphene could be used in the construction of a <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator" title="Space elevator">space elevator</a>. It could also be used to safely store hydrogen for use in a hydrogen based engine in cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glassy_carbon_and_a_1cm3_graphite_cube_HP68-79.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3674" data-file-width="4432" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Glassy_carbon_and_a_1cm3_graphite_cube_HP68-79.jpg/220px-Glassy_carbon_and_a_1cm3_graphite_cube_HP68-79.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Glassy_carbon_and_a_1cm3_graphite_cube_HP68-79.jpg/330px-Glassy_carbon_and_a_1cm3_graphite_cube_HP68-79.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Glassy_carbon_and_a_1cm3_graphite_cube_HP68-79.jpg/440px-Glassy_carbon_and_a_1cm3_graphite_cube_HP68-79.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glassy_carbon_and_a_1cm3_graphite_cube_HP68-79.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A large sample of glassy carbon</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorphous" title="Amorphous">amorphous</a> form is an assortment of carbon atoms in a non-crystalline, irregular, glassy state, not held in a crystalline macrostructure. It is present as a powder, and is the main constituent of substances such as <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lampblack" title="Lampblack">lampblack</a> (<a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a>. At normal pressures, carbon takes the form of graphite, in which each atom is bonded trigonally to three others in a plane composed of fused <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagonal</a> rings, just like those in <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The resulting network is 2-dimensional, and the resulting flat sheets are stacked and loosely bonded through weak <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">van der Waals forces</a>. This gives graphite its softness and its <a href="/wiki/Cleavage_(crystal)" title="Cleavage (crystal)">cleaving</a> properties (the sheets slip easily past one another). Because of the delocalization of one of the outer electrons of each atom to form a <a href="/wiki/Delocalized_electron" title="Delocalized electron">π-cloud</a>, graphite conducts <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, but only in the plane of each <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalently bonded</a> sheet. This results in a lower bulk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> for carbon than for most <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>. The delocalization also accounts for the energetic stability of graphite over diamond at room temperature.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eight_Allotropes_of_Carbon.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1895" data-file-width="1762" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Eight_Allotropes_of_Carbon.png/300px-Eight_Allotropes_of_Carbon.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Eight_Allotropes_of_Carbon.png/450px-Eight_Allotropes_of_Carbon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Eight_Allotropes_of_Carbon.png/600px-Eight_Allotropes_of_Carbon.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eight_Allotropes_of_Carbon.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some allotropes of carbon: a) <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>; b) <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>; c) <a href="/wiki/Lonsdaleite" title="Lonsdaleite">lonsdaleite</a>; d–f) <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerenes</a> (C<sub>60</sub>, C<sub>540</sub>, C<sub>70</sub>); g) <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a>; h) <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotube</a></div></div></div>
<p>At very high pressures, carbon forms the more compact allotrope, <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>, having nearly twice the density of graphite. Here, each atom is bonded <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedron" title="Tetrahedron">tetrahedrally</a> to four others, forming a 3-dimensional network of puckered six-membered rings of atoms. Diamond has the same <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">cubic structure</a> as <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, and because of the strength of the carbon-carbon <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bonds</a>, it is the hardest naturally occurring substance measured by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohs_scale" title="Mohs scale">resistance to scratching</a>. Contrary to the popular belief that <i>"diamonds are forever"</i>, they are thermodynamically unstable (Δ<sub>f</sub><i>G</i>°(diamond, 298 K) = 2.9 kJ/mol<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>) under normal conditions (298 K, 10<sup>5</sup> Pa) and transform into <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-therm_prop_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-therm_prop-17">[17]</a></sup> Due to a high activation energy barrier, the transition into graphite is so slow at normal temperature that it is unnoticeable.  The bottom left corner of the phase diagram for carbon has not been scrutinized experimentally.  However, a recent computational study employing <a href="/wiki/Density_functional_theory" title="Density functional theory">density functional theory</a> methods reached the conclusion that as <span class="nowrap"><i>T</i> → 0 K</span> and <span class="nowrap"><i>p</i> → 0 Pa</span>, diamond becomes <i>more stable</i> than graphite by approximately 1.1 kJ/mol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>  Under some conditions, carbon crystallizes as <a href="/wiki/Lonsdaleite" title="Lonsdaleite">lonsdaleite</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal" title="Hexagonal">hexagonal</a> <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystal</a> lattice with all atoms covalently bonded and properties similar to those of diamond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lonsdaletite_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-lonsdaletite-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">Fullerenes</a> are a synthetic crystalline formation with a graphite-like structure, but in place of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_crystal_system" title="Hexagonal crystal system">hexagons</a>, fullerenes are formed of pentagons (or even heptagons) of carbon atoms. The missing (or additional) atoms warp the sheets into spheres, ellipses, or cylinders. The properties of fullerenes (split into buckyballs, buckytubes, and nanobuds) have not yet been fully analyzed and represent an intense area of research in <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a>. The names <i>"fullerene"</i> and <i>"buckyball"</i> are given after <a href="/wiki/Buckminster_Fuller" title="Buckminster Fuller">Richard Buckminster Fuller</a>, popularizer of <a href="/wiki/Geodesic_dome" title="Geodesic dome">geodesic domes</a>, which resemble the structure of fullerenes. The buckyballs are fairly large molecules formed completely of carbon bonded trigonally, forming <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">spheroids</a> (the best-known and simplest is the soccerball-shaped C<sub>60</sub> <a href="/wiki/Buckminsterfullerene" title="Buckminsterfullerene">buckminsterfullerene</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buckyballs_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-buckyballs-29">[29]</a></sup> Carbon nanotubes are structurally similar to buckyballs, except that each atom is bonded trigonally in a curved sheet that forms a hollow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" title="Cylinder (geometry)">cylinder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nanotubes_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-nanotubes-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nanotubes2_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-nanotubes2-31">[31]</a></sup> Nanobuds were first reported in 2007 and are hybrid bucky tube/buckyball materials (buckyballs are covalently bonded to the outer wall of a nanotube) that combine the properties of both in a single structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nanobuds_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-nanobuds-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Of the other discovered allotropes, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanofoam" title="Carbon nanofoam">carbon nanofoam</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a> allotrope discovered in 1997. It consists of a low-density cluster-assembly of carbon atoms strung together in a loose three-dimensional web, in which the atoms are bonded trigonally in six- and seven-membered rings. It is among the lightest known solids, with a density of about 2 kg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Glassy_carbon" title="Glassy carbon">glassy carbon</a> contains a high proportion of closed <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">porosity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glassy_carbon_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-glassy_carbon-36">[36]</a></sup> but contrary to normal graphite, the graphitic layers are not stacked like pages in a book, but have a more random arrangement. <a href="/wiki/Linear_acetylenic_carbon" title="Linear acetylenic carbon">Linear acetylenic carbon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAC_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-LAC-38">[38]</a></sup> has the chemical structure<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAC_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-LAC-38">[38]</a></sup> −(C:::C)<sub><i>n</i></sub>−. Carbon in this modification is linear with <i>sp</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" title="Orbital hybridization">orbital hybridization</a>, and is a <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymer</a> with alternating single and triple bonds. This carbyne is of considerable interest to <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a> as its <a href="/wiki/Young%27s_modulus" title="Young's modulus">Young's modulus</a> is 40 times that of the hardest known material – diamond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, a team at the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_State_University" title="North Carolina State University">North Carolina State University</a> announced the development of another allotrope they have dubbed <a href="/wiki/Q-carbon" title="Q-carbon">Q-carbon</a>, created by a high energy low duration laser pulse on amorphous carbon dust. Q-carbon is reported to exhibit ferromagetism, <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a>, and a hardness superior to diamonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GraphiteOreUSGOV.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/GraphiteOreUSGOV.jpg/220px-GraphiteOreUSGOV.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/GraphiteOreUSGOV.jpg/330px-GraphiteOreUSGOV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/GraphiteOreUSGOV.jpg/440px-GraphiteOreUSGOV.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GraphiteOreUSGOV.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graphite ore, shown with a penny for scale</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rough_diamond.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="271" data-file-width="360" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Rough_diamond.jpg/220px-Rough_diamond.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Rough_diamond.jpg/330px-Rough_diamond.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Rough_diamond.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rough_diamond.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Raw diamond crystal</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1133" data-file-width="1687" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png/220px-WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png/330px-WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png/440px-WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Present day" (1990s) sea surface <a href="/wiki/Total_inorganic_carbon" title="Total inorganic carbon">dissolved inorganic carbon</a> concentration (from the <a href="/wiki/Global_Ocean_Data_Analysis_Project" title="Global Ocean Data Analysis Project">GLODAP</a> <a href="/wiki/Climatology" title="Climatology">climatology</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon is the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">fourth most abundant chemical element</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a> by mass after hydrogen, helium, and oxygen. Carbon is abundant in the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body%27s_atmosphere" title="Celestial body's atmosphere">atmospheres</a> of most <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-48">[48]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> contain microscopic diamonds that were formed when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">solar system</a> was still a <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lauretta_McSween_2006_p._199_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lauretta_McSween_2006_p._199-49">[49]</a></sup> Microscopic diamonds may also be formed by the intense pressure and high temperature at the sites of meteorite impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014 <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> announced a <a class="external text" href="http://www.astrochem.org/pahdb/" rel="nofollow">greatly upgraded database</a> for tracking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs) in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>. More than 20% of the carbon in the universe may be associated with PAHs, complex compounds of carbon and hydrogen without oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> These compounds figure in the <a href="/wiki/PAH_world_hypothesis" title="PAH world hypothesis">PAH world hypothesis</a> where they are hypothesized to have a role in <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a> and formation of <a href="/wiki/Life#Extraterrestrial_life" title="Life">life</a>. PAHs seem to have been formed "a couple of billion years" after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, are widespread throughout the universe, and are associated with <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">new stars</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exoplanets" title="Exoplanets">exoplanets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been estimated that the solid earth as a whole contains 730 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> of carbon, with 2000 ppm in the core and 120 ppm in the combined mantle and crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Since the mass of the earth is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7024597200000000000♠</span>5.972<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>24</sup> kg</span>, this would imply 4360 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigatonne" title="Gigatonne">gigatonnes</a> of carbon. This is much more than the amount of carbon in the oceans or atmosphere (below).
</p><p>In combination with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, carbon is found in the Earth's atmosphere (approximately 810 gigatonnes of carbon) and dissolved in all water bodies (approximately 36,000 gigatonnes of carbon). Around 1,900 gigatonnes of carbon are present in the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">Hydrocarbons</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>) contain carbon as well. <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> <a href="/wiki/Mineral_resource_classification" title="Mineral resource classification">"reserves" (not "resources")</a> amount to around 900 gigatonnes with perhaps 18,000 Gt of resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">Oil reserves</a> are around 150 gigatonnes. Proven sources of natural gas are about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7014175000000000000♠</span>175<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>12</sup> cubic metres</span>  (containing about 105 gigatonnes of carbon), but studies estimate another <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7014900000000000000♠</span>900<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>12</sup> cubic metres</span> of "unconventional" deposits such as <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a>, representing about 540 gigatonnes of carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon is also found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methane_hydrates" title="Methane hydrates">methane hydrates</a> in polar regions and under the seas. Various estimates put this carbon between 500, 2500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigatonne" title="Gigatonne">Gt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> or 3,000 Gt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the past, quantities of hydrocarbons were greater. According to one source, in the period from 1751 to 2008 about 347 gigatonnes of carbon were released as carbon dioxide to the atmosphere from burning of fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Another source puts the amount added to the atmosphere for the period since 1750 at 879 Gt, and the total going to the atmosphere, sea, and land (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peat_bogs" title="Peat bogs">peat bogs</a>) at almost 2,000 Gt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon is a constituent (about 12% by mass) of the very large masses of <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> rock (<a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> and so on). <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> is very rich in carbon (<a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite</a> contains 92–98%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and is the largest commercial source of mineral carbon, accounting for 4,000 gigatonnes or 80% of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>As for individual carbon allotropes, graphite is found in large quantities in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (mostly in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>), <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. Natural diamonds occur in the rock <a href="/wiki/Kimberlite" title="Kimberlite">kimberlite</a>, found in ancient <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> "necks", or "pipes". Most diamond deposits are in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, notably in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Republic of the Congo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>. Diamond deposits have also been found in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the Russian <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, and in Northern and Western <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. Diamonds are now also being recovered from the ocean floor off the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>. Diamonds are found naturally, but about 30% of all industrial diamonds used in the U.S. are now manufactured.
</p><p>Carbon-14 is formed in upper layers of the troposphere and the stratosphere at altitudes of 9–15 km by a reaction that is precipitated by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_neutron" title="Thermal neutron">Thermal neutrons</a> are produced that collide with the nuclei of nitrogen-14, forming carbon-14 and a proton. As such, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6988150000000000000♠</span>1.5%<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−10</sup></span> of atmospheric carbon dioxide contains carbon-14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon-rich asteroids are relatively preponderant in the outer parts of the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> in our <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">solar system</a>. These asteroids have not yet been directly sampled by scientists. The asteroids can be used in hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">space-based carbon mining</a>, which may be possible in the future, but is currently technologically impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_carbon" title="Isotopes of carbon">Isotopes of carbon</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">Isotopes</a> of carbon are <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> that contain six <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> plus a number of <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> (varying from 2 to 16). Carbon has two stable, naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isotopes_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-isotopes-14">[14]</a></sup> The isotope <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a> (<sup>12</sup>C) forms 98.93% of the carbon on Earth, while <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13">carbon-13</a> (<sup>13</sup>C) forms the remaining 1.07%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isotopes_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-isotopes-14">[14]</a></sup> The concentration of <sup>12</sup>C is further increased in biological materials because biochemical reactions discriminate against <sup>13</sup>C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1961, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) adopted the isotope <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a> as the basis for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Identification of carbon in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">nuclear magnetic resonance</a> (NMR) experiments is done with the isotope <sup>13</sup>C.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">Carbon-14</a> (<sup>14</sup>C) is a naturally occurring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotope</a>, created in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_atmosphere" title="Upper atmosphere">upper atmosphere</a> (lower <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a> and upper <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>) by interaction of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> with <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> It is found in trace amounts on Earth of 1 part per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/10%5E12" title="10^12">trillion</a> (0.0000000001%) or more, mostly confined to the atmosphere and superficial deposits, particularly of <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> and other organic materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> This isotope decays by 0.158 MeV <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">β<sup>−</sup> emission</a>. Because of its relatively short <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 5730 years, <sup>14</sup>C is virtually absent in ancient rocks. The amount of <sup>14</sup>C in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> and in living organisms is almost constant, but decreases predictably in their bodies after death. This principle is used in <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dating</a>, invented in 1949, which has been used extensively to determine the age of carbonaceous materials with ages up to about 40,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are 15 known isotopes of carbon and the shortest-lived of these is <sup>8</sup>C which decays through <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> and has a half-life of 1.98739x10<sup>−21</sup> s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The exotic <sup>19</sup>C exhibits a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_halo" title="Nuclear halo">nuclear halo</a>, which means its <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> is appreciably larger than would be expected if the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> were a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a> of constant <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_in_stars">Formation in stars</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Triple-alpha_process" title="Triple-alpha process">Triple-alpha process</a> and <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a></div>
<p>Formation of the carbon atomic nucleus occurs within a <a href="/wiki/Giant_star" title="Giant star">giant</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">supergiant</a> star through the <a href="/wiki/Triple-alpha_process" title="Triple-alpha process">triple-alpha process</a>. This requires a nearly simultaneous collision of three <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> (<a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> nuclei), as the products of further <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> reactions of helium with hydrogen or another helium nucleus produce <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_lithium" title="Isotopes of lithium">lithium-5</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_beryllium" title="Isotopes of beryllium">beryllium-8</a> respectively, both of which are highly unstable and decay almost instantly back into smaller nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Audi_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-72">[72]</a></sup> The triple-alpha process happens in conditions of temperatures over 100 megakelvin and helium concentration that the rapid expansion and cooling of the early universe prohibited, and therefore no significant carbon was created during the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.
</p><p>According to current physical cosmology theory, carbon is formed in the interiors of stars on the <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_branch" title="Horizontal branch">horizontal branch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostlie_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostlie-73">[73]</a></sup> When massive stars die as supernova, the carbon is scattered into space as dust. This dust becomes component material for the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">next-generation star</a> systems with accreted planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> is one such star system with an abundance of carbon, enabling the existence of life as we know it.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a> is an additional hydrogen fusion mechanism that powers stars, wherein carbon operates as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>.
</p><p>Rotational transitions of various isotopic forms of carbon monoxide (for example, <sup>12</sup>CO, <sup>13</sup>CO, and <sup>18</sup>CO) are detectable in the <a href="/wiki/Submillimetre_astronomy" title="Submillimetre astronomy">submillimeter</a> wavelength range, and are used in the study of <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">newly forming stars</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_clouds" title="Molecular clouds">molecular clouds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_cycle">Carbon cycle</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="417" data-file-width="540" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg/300px-Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg/450px-Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg/600px-Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of the carbon cycle. The black numbers indicate how much carbon is stored in various reservoirs, in billions tonnes ("GtC" stands for gigatonnes of carbon; figures are circa 2004). The purple numbers indicate how much carbon moves between reservoirs each year. The sediments, as defined in this diagram, do not include the ≈70 million GtC of carbonate rock and <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Under terrestrial conditions, conversion of one element to another is very rare. Therefore, the amount of carbon on Earth is effectively constant. Thus, processes that use carbon must obtain it from somewhere and dispose of it somewhere else. The paths of carbon in the environment form the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> plants draw <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> from the atmosphere (or seawater) and build it into biomass, as in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin cycle</a>, a process of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>. Some of this biomass is eaten by animals, while some carbon is exhaled by animals as carbon dioxide. The carbon cycle is considerably more complicated than this short loop; for example, some carbon dioxide is dissolved in the oceans; if bacteria do not consume it, dead plant or animal matter may become <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> or <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, which releases carbon when burned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_carbon" title="Compounds of carbon">Compounds of carbon</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_compounds">Organic compounds</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Methane-2D-stereo.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="512" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/150px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/225px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/300px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Methane-2D-stereo.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structural formula of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, the simplest possible organic compound.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3167" data-file-width="2722" height="349" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/300px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/450px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/600px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Correlation between the <i>carbon cycle</i> and formation of organic compounds. In plants, carbon dioxide formed by carbon fixation can join with water in <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> (<span style="color:green;">green</span>) to form organic compounds, which can be used and further converted by both plants and animals.</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon can form very long chains of interconnecting <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93carbon_bond" title="Carbon–carbon bond">carbon–carbon bonds</a>, a property that is called <a href="/wiki/Catenation" title="Catenation">catenation</a>. Carbon-carbon bonds are strong and stable. Through catenation, carbon forms a countless number of compounds. A tally of unique compounds shows that more contain carbon that those that do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burrows_Holman_Parsons_Pilling_2017_p._70_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burrows_Holman_Parsons_Pilling_2017_p._70-78">[78]</a></sup> A similar claim can be made for hydrogen because most organic compounds also contain hydrogen.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The simplest form of an organic molecule is the <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>—a large family of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a> that are composed of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> atoms bonded to a chain of carbon atoms. A hydrocarbon backbone can be substituted by other atoms, known as heteroatoms.  Common heteroatoms that appear in organic compounds include oxygen, nitrogen, sulfur, phosphorus, and the nonradioactive halogens, as well as the metals lithium and magnesium.  Organic compounds containing bonds to metal are known as organometallic compounds (<i>see below</i>).  Certain groupings of atoms, often including heteroatoms, recur in large numbers of organic compounds.  These collections, known as <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group"><i>functional groups</i></a>, confer common reactivity patterns and allow for the systematic study and categorization of organic compounds.  Chain length, shape and functional groups all affect the properties of organic molecules.
</p><p>In most stable compounds of carbon (and nearly all stable <i>organic</i> compounds), carbon obeys the <a href="/wiki/Octet_rule" title="Octet rule">octet rule</a> and is <i>tetravalent</i>, meaning that a carbon atom forms a total of four covalent bonds (which may include double and triple bonds).  Exceptions include a small number of stabilized <i>carbocations</i> (three bonds, positive charge), <i>radicals</i> (three bonds, neutral), <i>carbanions</i> (three bonds, negative charge) and <i>carbenes</i> (two bonds, neutral), although these species are much more likely to be encountered as unstable, reactive intermediates.    
</p><p>Carbon occurs in all known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic</a> life and is the basis of <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. When united with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, it forms various hydrocarbons that are important to industry as <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a>, as chemical feedstock for the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemicals</a>, and as <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.
</p><p>When combined with oxygen and hydrogen, carbon can form many groups of important biological compounds including <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lignan" title="Lignan">lignans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>, and aromatic <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a>. With <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> it forms <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloids</a>, and with the addition of sulfur also it forms <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubber" title="Rubber">rubber</a> products. With the addition of phosphorus to these other elements, it forms <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, the chemical-code carriers of life, and <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), the most important energy-transfer molecule in all living cells.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inorganic_compounds">Inorganic compounds</span></h3>
<p>Commonly carbon-containing compounds which are associated with minerals or which do not contain bonds to the other carbon atoms, halogens, or hydrogen, are treated separately from classical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">organic compounds</a>; the definition is not rigid, and the classification of some compounds can vary from author to author (see reference articles above). Among these are the simple oxides of carbon. The most prominent oxide is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>). This was once the principal constituent of the <a href="/wiki/Paleoatmosphere" title="Paleoatmosphere">paleoatmosphere</a>, but is a minor component of the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Dissolved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)" title="Water (molecule)">water</a>, it forms <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), but as most compounds with multiple single-bonded oxygens on a single carbon it is unstable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Through this intermediate, though, resonance-stabilized <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> are produced. Some important minerals are carbonates, notably <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">Carbon disulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CS<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) is similar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood289_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood289-22">[22]</a></sup>  Nevertheless, due to its physical properties and its association with organic synthesis, carbon disulfide is sometimes classified as an <i>organic</i> solvent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other common oxide is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO). It is formed by incomplete combustion, and is a colorless, odorless gas. The molecules each contain a triple bond and are fairly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">polar</a>, resulting in a tendency to bind permanently to hemoglobin molecules, displacing oxygen, which has a lower binding affinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">Cyanide</a> (CN<sup>−</sup>), has a similar structure, but behaves much like a <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halide</a> ion (<a href="/wiki/Pseudohalogen" title="Pseudohalogen">pseudohalogen</a>). For example, it can form the nitride <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen" title="Cyanogen">cyanogen</a> molecule ((CN)<sub>2</sub>), similar to diatomic halides. Likewise, the heavier analog of cyanide, <a href="/wiki/Cyaphide" title="Cyaphide">cyaphide</a> (CP<sup>−</sup>), is also considered inorganic, though most simple derivatives are highly unstable.  Other uncommon oxides are <a href="/wiki/Carbon_suboxide" title="Carbon suboxide">carbon suboxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> the unstable <a href="/wiki/Dicarbon_monoxide" title="Dicarbon monoxide">dicarbon monoxide</a> (C<sub>2</sub>O),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_trioxide" title="Carbon trioxide">carbon trioxide</a> (CO<sub>3</sub>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentanepentone" title="Cyclopentanepentone">cyclopentanepentone</a> (C<sub>5</sub>O<sub>5</sub>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fatiadi_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-fatiadi-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexanehexone" title="Cyclohexanehexone">cyclohexanehexone</a> (C<sub>6</sub>O<sub>6</sub>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fatiadi_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-fatiadi-89">[89]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mellitic_anhydride" title="Mellitic anhydride">mellitic anhydride</a> (C<sub>12</sub>O<sub>9</sub>).  However, mellitic anhydride is the triple acyl anhydride of mellitic acid; moreover, it contains a benzene ring. Thus, many chemists consider it to be organic.
</p><p>With reactive <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>, carbon forms either <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbides</a> (C<sup>4−</sup>) or <a href="/wiki/Acetylide" title="Acetylide">acetylides</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) to form alloys with high melting points. These anions are also associated with <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, both very weak <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>. With an electronegativity of 2.5,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> carbon prefers to form <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>. A few carbides are covalent lattices, like <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">carborundum</a> (SiC), which resembles <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>. Nevertheless, even the most polar and salt-like of carbides are not completely ionic compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood297_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood297-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organometallic_compounds">Organometallic compounds</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_chemistry" title="Organometallic chemistry">Organometallic chemistry</a></div>
<p>Organometallic compounds by definition contain at least one carbon-metal covalent bond. A wide range of such compounds exist; major classes include simple alkyl-metal compounds (for example, <a href="/wiki/Tetraethyllead" title="Tetraethyllead">tetraethyllead</a>), η<sup>2</sup>-alkene compounds (for example, <a href="/wiki/Zeise%27s_salt" title="Zeise's salt">Zeise's salt</a>), and η<sup>3</sup>-allyl compounds (for example, <a href="/wiki/Allylpalladium_chloride_dimer" title="Allylpalladium chloride dimer">allylpalladium chloride dimer</a>); <a href="/wiki/Metallocene" title="Metallocene">metallocenes</a> containing cyclopentadienyl ligands (for example, <a href="/wiki/Ferrocene" title="Ferrocene">ferrocene</a>); and <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal_carbene_complex" title="Transition metal carbene complex">transition metal carbene complexes</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl" title="Metal carbonyl">metal carbonyls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanometalate" title="Cyanometalate">metal cyanides</a> exist (for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetracarbonylnickel" title="Tetracarbonylnickel">tetracarbonylnickel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_ferricyanide" title="Potassium ferricyanide">potassium ferricyanide</a>); some workers consider metal carbonyl and cyanide complexes without other carbon ligands to be purely inorganic, and not organometallic.  However, most organometallic chemists consider metal complexes with any carbon ligand, even 'inorganic carbon' (e.g., carbonyls, cyanides, and certain types of carbides and acetylides) to be organometallic in nature.  Metal complexes containing organic ligands without a carbon-metal covalent bond (e.g., metal carboxylates) are termed <i>metalorganic</i> compounds.
</p><p>While carbon is understood to strongly prefer formation of four covalent bonds, other exotic bonding schemes are also known.  An interesting compound containing an octahedral hexacoordinated carbon atom has been reported. The cation of the compound is [(Ph<sub>3</sub>PAu)<sub>6</sub>C]<sup>2+</sup>. This phenomenon has been attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Aurophilicity" title="Aurophilicity">aurophilicity</a> of the gold ligands, which provide additional stabilization of an otherwise labile species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>  In nature, the iron-molybdenum cofactor (<a href="/wiki/FeMoco" title="FeMoco">FeMoco</a>) responsible for microbial <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a> likewise has an octahedral carbon center (formally a carbide, C(-IV)) bonded to six iron atoms.  In 2016, it was confirmed that, in line with earlier theoretical predictions, <a href="/wiki/Hexamethylbenzene" title="Hexamethylbenzene">hexamethylbenzene dication</a> contains a carbon atom with six bonds, with the formulation [MeC(η<sup>5</sup>-C<sub>5</sub>Me<sub>5</sub>)]<sup>2+</sup>, making it an "organic <a href="/wiki/Metallocene" title="Metallocene">metallocene</a>".  Thus, a MeC<sup>3+</sup> fragment is bonded to a η<sup>5</sup>-C<sub>5</sub>Me<sub>5</sub><sup>−</sup> fragment through all five of the carbons of the ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is important to note that in the cases above, each of the bonds to carbon contain less than two formal electron pairs, making them hypercoordinate, but not hypervalent.  Even in cases of alleged 10-C-5 species (that is, a carbon with five ligands and a formal electron count of ten), as reported by Akiba and co-workers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> electronic structure calculations conclude that the total number of electrons around carbon is still less than eight, as in the case of other compounds described by <a href="/wiki/Three-center_bond" title="Three-center bond">three-center bonding</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_etymology">History and etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoine_lavoisier.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="318" data-file-width="303" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg/170px-Antoine_lavoisier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg/255px-Antoine_lavoisier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoine_lavoisier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> in his youth</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> name <i>carbon</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>carbo</i> for coal and charcoal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> whence also comes the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i>charbon</i>, meaning charcoal. In <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>, the names for carbon are <i>Kohlenstoff</i>, <i>koolstof</i> and <i>kulstof</i> respectively, all literally meaning <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>-substance.
</p><p>Carbon was discovered in prehistory and was known in the forms of <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> to the earliest <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilizations</a>.
