Diamonds were known probably as early as 2500 BCE in China, while carbon in the form of <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> was made around Roman times by the same chemistry as it is today, by heating wood in a <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramid</a> covered with <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> to exclude air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ancient_China_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-ancient_China-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="692" data-file-width="588" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png/170px-Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png/255px-Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png/340px-Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1722, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Antoine_Ferchault_de_R%C3%A9aumur" title="René Antoine Ferchault de Réaumur">René Antoine Ferchault de Réaumur</a> demonstrated that iron was transformed into steel through the absorption of some substance, now known to be carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In 1772, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> showed that diamonds are a form of carbon; when he burned samples of charcoal and diamond and found that neither produced any water and that both released the same amount of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> per <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a>.
In 1779,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> showed that graphite, which had been thought of as a form of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, was instead identical with charcoal but with a small admixture of iron, and that it gave "aerial acid" (his name for carbon dioxide) when oxidized with nitric acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In 1786, the French scientists <a href="/wiki/Claude_Louis_Berthollet" title="Claude Louis Berthollet">Claude Louis Berthollet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaspard_Monge" title="Gaspard Monge">Gaspard Monge</a> and C. A. Vandermonde confirmed that graphite was mostly carbon by oxidizing it in oxygen in much the same way Lavoisier had done with diamond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Some iron again was left, which the French scientists thought was necessary to the graphite structure. In their publication they proposed the name <i>carbone</i> (Latin <i>carbonum</i>) for the element in graphite which was given off as a gas upon burning graphite. Antoine Lavoisier then listed carbon as an <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> in his 1789 textbook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>A new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotrope" title="Allotrope">allotrope</a> of carbon, <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerene</a>, that was discovered in 1985<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> includes <a href="/wiki/Nanostructure" title="Nanostructure">nanostructured</a> forms such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buckyball" title="Buckyball">buckyballs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">nanotubes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buckyballs_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-buckyballs-29">[29]</a></sup> Their discoverers – <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curl" title="Robert Curl">Robert Curl</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harold_Kroto" title="Harold Kroto">Harold Kroto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Smalley" title="Richard Smalley">Richard Smalley</a> – received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in Chemistry in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The resulting renewed interest in new forms lead to the discovery of further exotic allotropes, including <a href="/wiki/Glassy_carbon" title="Glassy carbon">glassy carbon</a>, and the realization that "<a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a>" is not strictly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorphous" title="Amorphous">amorphous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glassy_carbon_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-glassy_carbon-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graphite">Graphite</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">Graphite</a></div>
<p>Commercially viable natural deposits of graphite occur in many parts of the world, but the most important sources economically are in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. Graphite deposits are of <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic</a> origin, found in association with <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">mica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspars</a> in schists, <a href="/wiki/Gneiss" title="Gneiss">gneisses</a> and metamorphosed <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> as <a href="/wiki/Lens_(geology)" title="Lens (geology)">lenses</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vein_(geology)" title="Vein (geology)">veins</a>, sometimes of a metre or more in thickness. Deposits of graphite in <a href="/wiki/Borrowdale" title="Borrowdale">Borrowdale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumberland" title="Cumberland">Cumberland</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> were at first of sufficient size and purity that, until the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Pencil" title="Pencil">pencils</a> were made simply by sawing blocks of natural graphite into strips before encasing the strips in wood. Today, smaller deposits of graphite are obtained by crushing the parent rock and floating the lighter graphite out on water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are three types of natural graphite—amorphous, flake or crystalline flake, and vein or lump. Amorphous graphite is the lowest quality and most abundant. Contrary to science, in industry "amorphous" refers to very small crystal size rather than complete lack of crystal structure. Amorphous is used for lower value graphite products and is the lowest priced graphite. Large amorphous graphite deposits are found in China, Europe, Mexico and the United States. Flake graphite is less common and of higher quality than amorphous; it occurs as separate plates that crystallized in metamorphic rock. Flake graphite can be four times the price of amorphous. Good quality flakes can be processed into expandable graphite for many uses, such as <a href="/wiki/Flame_retardant" title="Flame retardant">flame retardants</a>. The foremost deposits are found in Austria, Brazil, Canada, China, Germany and Madagascar. Vein or lump graphite is the rarest, most valuable, and highest quality type of natural graphite. It occurs in veins along intrusive contacts in solid lumps, and it is only commercially mined in Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USGS" title="USGS">USGS</a>, world production of natural graphite was 1.1 million tonnes in 2010, to which China contributed 800,000 t, India 130,000 t, Brazil 76,000 t, North Korea 30,000 t and Canada 25,000 t. No natural graphite was reported mined in the United States, but 118,000 t of synthetic graphite with an estimated value of $998 million was produced in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diamond">Diamond</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">Diamond</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png/440px-Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png/660px-Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png/880px-Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diamond output in 2005</div></div></div>
<p>The diamond supply chain is controlled by a limited number of powerful businesses, and is also highly concentrated in a small number of locations around the world (see figure).
</p><p>Only a very small fraction of the diamond ore consists of actual diamonds. The ore is crushed, during which care has to be taken in order to prevent larger diamonds from being destroyed in this process and subsequently the particles are sorted by density. Today, diamonds are located in the diamond-rich density fraction with the help of <a href="/wiki/X-ray_fluorescence" title="X-ray fluorescence">X-ray fluorescence</a>, after which the final sorting steps are done by hand. Before the use of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> became commonplace, the separation was done with grease belts; diamonds have a stronger tendency to stick to grease than the other minerals in the ore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically diamonds were known to be found only in alluvial deposits in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_India" title="Southern India">southern India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catelle1_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Catelle1-107">[107]</a></sup> India led the world in diamond production from the time of their discovery in approximately the 9th century BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ball_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-108">[108]</a></sup> to the mid-18th century AD, but the commercial potential of these sources had been exhausted by the late 18th century and at that time India was eclipsed by Brazil where the first non-Indian diamonds were found in 1725.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diamond production of primary deposits (kimberlites and lamproites) only started in the 1870s after the discovery of the Diamond fields in South Africa. Production has increased over time and now an accumulated total of 4.5 billion carats have been mined since that date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-giasummer2007_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-giasummer2007-110">[110]</a></sup> About 20% of that amount has been mined in the last 5 years alone, and during the last ten years 9 new mines have started production while 4 more are waiting to be opened soon. Most of these mines are located in Canada, Zimbabwe, Angola, and one in Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-giasummer2007_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-giasummer2007-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, diamonds have been found in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DGemGLorenz_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-DGemGLorenz-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In 2004, a startling discovery of a microscopic diamond in the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> led to the January 2008 bulk-sampling of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kimberlite_pipes" title="Kimberlite pipes">kimberlite pipes</a> in a remote part of <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, most commercially viable diamond deposits are in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In 2005, Russia produced almost one-fifth of the global diamond output, reports the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a>. Australia has the richest diamantiferous pipe with production reaching peak levels of 42 metric tons (41 long tons; 46 short tons) per year in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DGemGLorenz_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-DGemGLorenz-111">[111]</a></sup> There are also commercial deposits being actively mined in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a> of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> (mostly in <a href="/wiki/Sakha_Republic" title="Sakha Republic">Yakutia territory</a>; for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mir_Mine" title="Mir Mine">Mir pipe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Udachnaya_pipe" title="Udachnaya pipe">Udachnaya pipe</a>), Brazil, and in Northern and Western <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mechanical_pencil_lead_spilling_out_051907.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="583" data-file-width="785" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mechanical_pencil_lead_spilling_out_051907.jpg/220px-Mechanical_pencil_lead_spilling_out_051907.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mechanical_pencil_lead_spilling_out_051907.jpg/330px-Mechanical_pencil_lead_spilling_out_051907.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Mechanical_pencil_lead_spilling_out_051907.jpg/440px-Mechanical_pencil_lead_spilling_out_051907.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mechanical_pencil_lead_spilling_out_051907.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pencil leads for mechanical pencils are made of <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> (often mixed with a clay or synthetic binder).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charcoal_sticks_051907.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="810" data-file-width="1080" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Charcoal_sticks_051907.jpg/220px-Charcoal_sticks_051907.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Charcoal_sticks_051907.jpg/330px-Charcoal_sticks_051907.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Charcoal_sticks_051907.jpg/440px-Charcoal_sticks_051907.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charcoal_sticks_051907.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sticks of vine and compressed <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kohlenstofffasermatte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Kohlenstofffasermatte.jpg/220px-Kohlenstofffasermatte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Kohlenstofffasermatte.jpg/330px-Kohlenstofffasermatte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Kohlenstofffasermatte.jpg/440px-Kohlenstofffasermatte.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kohlenstofffasermatte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cloth of woven carbon fibres</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SiC_p1390066.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1206" data-file-width="1419" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/220px-SiC_p1390066.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/330px-SiC_p1390066.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/440px-SiC_p1390066.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SiC_p1390066.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">Silicon carbide</a> <a href="/wiki/Single_crystal" title="Single crystal">single crystal</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C60-Fulleren-kristallin.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="614" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/C60-Fulleren-kristallin.JPG/220px-C60-Fulleren-kristallin.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/C60-Fulleren-kristallin.JPG/330px-C60-Fulleren-kristallin.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/C60-Fulleren-kristallin.JPG/440px-C60-Fulleren-kristallin.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C60-Fulleren-kristallin.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>C</i><sub>60</sub> fullerene in crystalline form</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_carbide.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="864" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Tungsten_carbide.jpg/220px-Tungsten_carbide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Tungsten_carbide.jpg/330px-Tungsten_carbide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Tungsten_carbide.jpg/440px-Tungsten_carbide.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_carbide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tungsten_carbide" title="Tungsten carbide">Tungsten carbide</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endmills" title="Endmills">endmills</a></div></div></div>
<p>Carbon is essential to all known living systems, and without it life as we know it could not exist (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alternative_biochemistry" title="Alternative biochemistry">alternative biochemistry</a>). The major economic use of carbon other than food and wood is in the form of hydrocarbons, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> gas and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> (petroleum). <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">Crude oil</a> is <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distilled</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">refineries</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical_industry" title="Petrochemical industry">petrochemical industry</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>, and other products. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">Cellulose</a> is a natural, carbon-containing polymer produced by plants in the form of <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">Cellulose</a> is used primarily for maintaining structure in plants. Commercially valuable carbon polymers of animal origin include <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cashmere_wool" title="Cashmere wool">cashmere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastics" title="Plastics">Plastics</a> are made from synthetic carbon polymers, often with oxygen and nitrogen atoms included at regular intervals in the main polymer chain. The raw materials for many of these synthetic substances come from crude oil.
</p><p>The uses of carbon and its compounds are extremely varied. It can form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alloys" title="Alloys">alloys</a> with <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, of which the most common is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_steel" title="Carbon steel">carbon steel</a>. <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">Graphite</a> is combined with <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clays</a> to form the 'lead' used in <a href="/wiki/Pencil" title="Pencil">pencils</a> used for <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drawing" title="Drawing">drawing</a>. It is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricant</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a>, as a molding material in <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> manufacture, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrodes" title="Electrodes">electrodes</a> for dry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electroforming" title="Electroforming">electroforming</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Brush_(electric)" title="Brush (electric)">brushes</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_motors" title="Electric motors">electric motors</a> and as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactors" title="Nuclear reactors">nuclear reactors</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">Charcoal</a> is used as a drawing material in <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">artwork</a>, barbecue <a href="/wiki/Grilling" title="Grilling">grilling</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_smelting" title="Iron smelting">iron smelting</a>, and in many other applications. Wood, coal and oil are used as <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> for production of energy and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heating" title="Heating">heating</a>. Gem quality <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> is used in jewelry, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_diamond" title="Industrial diamond">industrial diamonds</a> are used in drilling, cutting and polishing tools for machining metals and stone. Plastics are made from fossil hydrocarbons, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" title="Carbon fiber">carbon fiber</a>, made by <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> of synthetic <a href="/wiki/Polyester" title="Polyester">polyester</a> <a href="/wiki/Fiber" title="Fiber">fibers</a> is used to reinforce plastics to form advanced, lightweight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_materials" title="Composite materials">composite materials</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" title="Carbon fiber">Carbon fiber</a> is made by pyrolysis of extruded and stretched filaments of <a href="/wiki/Polyacrylonitrile" title="Polyacrylonitrile">polyacrylonitrile</a> (PAN) and other organic substances. The crystallographic structure and mechanical properties of the fiber depend on the type of starting material, and on the subsequent processing. Carbon fibers made from PAN have structure resembling narrow filaments of graphite, but thermal processing may re-order the structure into a continuous rolled sheet. The result is fibers with higher <a href="/wiki/Specific_strength" title="Specific strength">specific tensile strength</a> than steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cantwell_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-cantwell-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">Carbon black</a> is used as the black <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> in <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">ink</a>, artist's oil paint and water colours, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_paper" title="Carbon paper">carbon paper</a>, automotive finishes, <a href="/wiki/India_ink" title="India ink">India ink</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_printer" title="Laser printer">laser printer</a> <a href="/wiki/Toner" title="Toner">toner</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">Carbon black</a> is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Filler_(materials)" title="Filler (materials)">filler</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubber" title="Rubber">rubber</a> products such as tyres and in <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastic</a> compounds. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Activated_charcoal" title="Activated charcoal">Activated charcoal</a> is used as an <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(chemistry)" title="Absorption (chemistry)">absorbent</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adsorbent" title="Adsorbent">adsorbent</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filter_(chemistry)" title="Filter (chemistry)">filter</a> material in applications as diverse as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_masks" title="Gas masks">gas masks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_purification" title="Water purification">water purification</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kitchen" title="Kitchen">kitchen</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extractor_hood" title="Extractor hood">extractor hoods</a>, and in medicine to <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(chemistry)" title="Absorption (chemistry)">absorb</a> toxins, poisons, or gases from the <a href="/wiki/Human_digestive_system" title="Human digestive system">digestive system</a>. Carbon is used in <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">chemical reduction</a> at high temperatures. <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">Coke</a> is used to reduce iron ore into iron (smelting). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Case_hardening" title="Case hardening">Case hardening</a> of steel is achieved by heating finished steel components in carbon powder. <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">Carbides</a> of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_carbide" title="Tungsten carbide">tungsten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boron_carbide" title="Boron carbide">boron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titanium_carbide" title="Titanium carbide">titanium</a>, are among the hardest known materials, and are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrasives" title="Abrasives">abrasives</a> in cutting and grinding tools. Carbon compounds make up most of the materials used in clothing, such as natural and synthetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Textiles" title="Textiles">textiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a>, and almost all of the interior surfaces in the <a href="/wiki/Built_environment" title="Built environment">built environment</a> other than glass, stone and metal.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diamonds">Diamonds</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> industry falls into two categories: one dealing with gem-grade diamonds and the other, with industrial-grade diamonds. While a large trade in both types of diamonds exists, the two markets act in dramatically different ways.
</p><p>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> or <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, gem diamonds do not trade as a <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodity</a>: there is a substantial mark-up in the sale of diamonds, and there is not a very active market for resale of diamonds.
</p><p>Industrial diamonds are valued mostly for their hardness and heat conductivity, with the gemological qualities of clarity and color being mostly irrelevant. About 80% of mined diamonds (equal to about 100 million carats or 20 tonnes annually) are unsuitable for use as gemstones are relegated for industrial use (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Bort" title="Bort">bort</a>)</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_diamond" title="Synthetic diamond">synthetic diamonds</a>, invented in the 1950s, found almost immediate industrial applications; 3 billion carats (600 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>) of synthetic diamond is produced annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>The dominant industrial use of diamond is in cutting, drilling, grinding, and polishing. Most of these applications do not require large diamonds; in fact, most diamonds of gem-quality except for their small size can be used industrially. Diamonds are embedded in drill tips or saw blades, or ground into a powder for use in grinding and polishing applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Specialized applications include use in laboratories as containment for <a href="/wiki/Pressure_experiment" title="Pressure experiment">high pressure experiments</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Diamond_anvil_cell" title="Diamond anvil cell">diamond anvil cell</a>), high-performance <a href="/wiki/Bearing_(mechanical)" title="Bearing (mechanical)">bearings</a>, and limited use in specialized <a href="/wiki/Window" title="Window">windows</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> With the continuing advances in the production of synthetic diamonds, new applications are becoming feasible. Garnering much excitement is the possible use of diamond as a <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> suitable for <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">microchips</a>, and because of its exceptional heat conductance property, as a <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a> in <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6043" data-file-width="4544" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg/170px-Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg/255px-Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg/340px-Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worker at <a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">carbon black</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Sunray,_Texas" title="Sunray, Texas">Sunray, Texas</a> (photo by <a href="/wiki/John_Vachon" title="John Vachon">John Vachon</a>, 1942)</div></div></div><p>Pure carbon has extremely low <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> to humans and can be handled and even ingested safely in the form of graphite or charcoal.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="scientific information w/o source (October 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is resistant to dissolution or chemical attack, even in the acidic contents of the digestive tract. Consequently, once it enters into the body's tissues it is likely to remain there indefinitely. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">Carbon black</a> was probably one of the first pigments to be used for <a href="/wiki/Tattoo" title="Tattoo">tattooing</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi_the_Iceman" title="Ötzi the Iceman">Ötzi the Iceman</a> was found to have carbon tattoos that survived during his life and for 5200 years after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Inhalation of coal dust or soot (carbon black) in large quantities can be dangerous, irritating lung tissues and causing the congestive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_lung" title="Human lung">lung</a> disease, <a href="/wiki/Coalworker%27s_pneumoconiosis" title="Coalworker's pneumoconiosis">coalworker's pneumoconiosis</a>. Diamond dust used as an abrasive can be harmful if ingested or inhaled. Microparticles of carbon are produced in diesel engine exhaust fumes, and may accumulate in the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In these examples, the harm may result from contaminants (e.g., organic chemicals, heavy metals) rather than from the carbon itself.
</p><p>Carbon generally has low toxicity to <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a>; but carbon nanoparticles are deadly to <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon may burn vigorously and brightly in the presence of air at high temperatures. Large accumulations of coal, which have remained inert for hundreds of millions of years in the absence of oxygen, may <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_combustion" title="Spontaneous combustion">spontaneously combust</a> when exposed to air in coal mine waste tips, ship cargo holds and coal bunkers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> and storage dumps.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear applications</a> where graphite is used as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a>, accumulation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wigner_energy" title="Wigner energy">Wigner energy</a> followed by a sudden, spontaneous release may occur. <a href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">Annealing</a> to at least 250 °C can release the energy safely, although in the <a href="/wiki/Windscale_fire" title="Windscale fire">Windscale fire</a> the procedure went wrong, causing other reactor materials to combust.
</p><p>The great variety of carbon compounds include such lethal poisons as <a href="/wiki/Tetrodotoxin" title="Tetrodotoxin">tetrodotoxin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lectin" title="Lectin">lectin</a> <a href="/wiki/Ricin" title="Ricin">ricin</a> from seeds of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castor_oil_plant" title="Castor oil plant">castor oil plant</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ricinus_communis" title="Ricinus communis">Ricinus communis</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a> (CN<sup>−</sup>), and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">carbon monoxide</a>; and such essentials to life as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>.
</p>
